རྣ*མཚུངས་ བོད་ཡིག ་ ཐོག་ལོ་རྒྱུས་ཟུར་ཙམ་གསལ་བ་མཁས་ ་

（ སྨོན་ཚིག་མདོར་བསྡུས)
ལ་བའི་བཀའ་དང་བསྟན་འཛིན་སྐྱེས་བུ་ཡིས། དདགོངས• •
བད་དང་མཐུན་འཛམ་གླིང་ཤས་ནི་བའི། [ཚོ་པཎ་མཁས་

པ•མ།
1ལེགས་ བཤད་མཁས་པ ་ དགའ་ བའི་སྒྲུང་ཡིག་འདི། །
ཡིད་མིས་དད་པས་ཀན་བཟང་སྟོབས་རྒྱལ་ནས།
བཅུ་དྲུག་ས་མོ་མེ་ལུག་ལོ།
།སྤྱི་ལོ་ཆིག་སྟོང་དགུ་བརྒྱ་དོན་
དག་ལ། །རྒྱུ ་ གར་རྒྱལ་ས་ ལི • ལིའི་གྲོང་ཁྱེར ''དུ། ]སྤར་དུ་
བསྐྲུན་པའི༔ རྣམ་དཀར་དགེ་བའི་མཐུས། །དཔལ་ ལྡན ་ འབྲུག་
པའི་ཆབ་ •སྲིད་བརྟན་པ་དང་། །མངའ་འབངས་བདེ་སྐྱིད་ ་

Introduction to the Gesar Epic
R. A, STEIN
College de France, Paris
The epic of Ge-sar, king of gLing, is widely spread all over
Tibet and neigbouring countries touched by Tibetan lamaist culture.
In Tibet it is known by everybody in many versions, either written
(in print or in manuscripts) or oral, from Amdo and Khams to
Central Tibet and Ladakh. Apart from the Tibetan language (in
classical written form and in various dialects), the story has also
spread in the shortened form of folk-stories in other languages :
lepcba (in Sikkim), balti (at the frontiers of Ladakh and Pakistan,
written down in Tibetan or in Persian script) and bursbaski, a langu-
age of Gilgit. But it is in Mongolian that the story has been known
for centuries in many versions, written and oral, as translations or
as adaptations, in classical written language and in various dialects
(especially khalsa, kalmuk and buriat).
It was through these Mongolian versions of Geser Khan that the
story has first been known in Europe through translations (1836-39).
Up to the present days the Mongolian versions have been studied
by many scholars more extensively than the Tibetan oncs, significant
contributions having been made in the fields of linguistics, literature,
folklore and history.
For a long time the Tibetan versions have been known in Europe
only by some oral texts written down in ladakhi dialect and by
(1)



summaries or short extracts. Only in 1956 the first three chapters
of the printed edition of gLing-tshang have been published by the
present writer in latin transcription. Since the arrival of the Tibetan
refugees in India and their most useful activity of publications of
Tibetan texts some chapters of the Ge-sar epic have been published.
But there are still many manuscripts and some printed texts hidden
in private and public libraries and awaiting publication.
ject of Mr Kunzang Tobgyel and Mani Dorji—to publish a large
collection of various chapters in various versions of the Ge-sar
cpic is therefore most welcome and shall provide an indispensable
material for future studies.
The pro-
The oral versions are spread by professional singers or bards
(sgrung-mkhan) who wander all over the country. Some may know
the whole or a great many chapters of the epic, others only some
of them. If invited for recitation, they can recite and sing for days
and weeks. They are said to be inspired, when singing, or even in
a kind of rapture or trance (sgrung 'bab). In any case their attitude
at that moment has largely a religious tint. They wear a special
hat, the parts of which are symbolically explained in a special song
(zhva-bshad) in the same way as do other specialists or on other
occasions (the "clown" 'dre-dkar chanting at the New Year; the
ritual "explanations" of every ingredient or instrument, like the
sword (gri-bshad), the horse, etc., various not specifically
buddhist rituals). The hat of the bard is caracterised by two big
asses or kyang ears, by sun and moon on the forehead and by
flower-like ornaments made of little shells (cowries).
The recitation resembles that of the theater.. The main story is
quickly told in prose in a kind of psalmody. It is frequently inter-
rupted by dialogues between the persons who appear in the story.
(2)



These are in reality an exchange of long songs (glu) chanted in
various melodies (called "horse", rta) in which each person first
presents himself as well as his weapons, his horse, etc. This is done,
as in the theater, by means of a stereotyped formula like : "do you
know me ? If you don't, I am so and so",
Many Tibetans know the general events of the story and can re-
cite the most famous songs, in the same way as people in the West
song the aria of the opera. Story-tellers also can resume the epic
shortly in the form of a folk-tale. So some versions can be very
complete, others may be shortened and preserve only parts of the
whole.
It is remarkable that the written versions, manuscripts or printed
editions, are composed in the same way as the oral recitals. The
main story, telling the events, is written in prose, the dialogues in
the form of alternate songs in verse.
The latter mostly occupy a
large place, wbereas the former are rather short. The two parts are
frequently distinguished by a different style of cursive script.
Even in the written versions, the songs (glu) start with excla-
mations like a-la a-la tha-la-la, etc. which clearly imitate the
oral recitation. One may thus presume that the oral versions are
primary in regard to the written ones. But this does not imply
that the epic in its present form, the only one we know-is a
popular" creation. The traditon of composing songs (mgur)-
often imitating those sung at "popular” festivals-is well esta-
blished in the learned and educated circles of great saints and
ecclesiastics like Mi-la ras-pa or 'Brug-pa Kun-legs. As we know
it now in all its available versions, the epic has certainly be
composed by a clerical author belonging to the type of inspired
poet-saint or yogin (especially in the bka’-brgyud-pa and the
( 3 )



roying-ma-pa orders). On the other hand, that does not mean, of
course, that such an author (who remains unknown) has not used
all kinds of folk-tales or local legends.
It is clear from the written versions that the long epic as we
have it now has incorporated or anyhow has preserved fragments
of a more or less independent and possibly popular" cycle. In fact
they often quote a story devoted to the deeds of the hero (ao Ge-
sar, but the boy Jo-ru) in the first days or years of his life. These
fragments in verse are written in a peculiar style, different from the
rest. But so far nothing is known about their origin or source.
The present epic is built on a clear-cut dichotomy in the life of
the main hero. On the one hand, in his youth (up to the age of 13
or 15 years) he is called Jo-ru (and variants; a name nobody under-
stands, though the written versions try some etymologies). He is
an ugly, nasty boy, dispised and ridiculised, but invested with
supernatural powers enabling him to play very bad practical jokes
on others. Only in the second part of bis life, when he is enthroned
as king of gLing is he called Ge-sar, endowed with glory and
might, a kind of cakravartin subduing demons and ennemies in ali
kinds of neighbouring countries. The first part resembles many
stories known all over the world of the type called "divine trick-
ster". One could be tempted to believe that this part of the story
may have had a separate existence before being used in the epic.
But such an hypothesis is far from necessary. The opposition
between the two ways of life of the hero can well be the essential
theme of the story. Before revealing himself in all his glory, the
hero-saint must undergo a sort of trial, a period of obscurity and
humiliation. Such a conception is well known through various
stories. It is at any rate the idea the clerical author of the present
( 4 )



epic had in mind.
For him, as is clearly stated, the period of ob-
scurity is the necessary preparation for the future achievements. It
creates the causes and forbodings of the future (in la naist theory :
rten-'brel 'grigs).
Anyhow, the presently known versions of the Ge-sar epic ali
agree on the general lines of the story and the sequence of events
at least for the 5 or 6 first chapters (le'u), each forming one or two
volumes in the written form. The first chapter is sometimes divided
into two parts, but the first of these parts has never come to light
so far as a separate text and is only known through fragments and
quotations. This is the story about the origin or creation, not of
the world at large, but of the country of men par excellence, called
gLing, where the story starts (Srid-gling). The country mostly ori-
gidates from the body of a giant or demon who is killed or subdued.
In spite of being a specific country, it is simply 'the country of men"
(mi-yul). Though gLing is the name of a principality in Khams,
as we shall see later, the name is explained in the epic itself as a
shortened form of 'dzam-bu-gling, i.e. jam budvípa, the southern
continent of men in buddbist cosmology. The second part of the
introductory story, mostly edited as the first chapter (Cha-gling)
deals with the situation prevailing before the arrival of the hero.
Everything goes wrong, ennemies in the forms of demons cause all
kinds of evil, the people are without a pcoper sovereign able to
maintain order and justice. Therefore a dean is sent to heaven to
ask the gods to send one of them to gLing in order to become its
king. This intervention is undertaken in the form of a bsang ritual
at a sacred mountain. It is significant that this theme is already
well attested for the origin of the Tibetan kings in the old inscrip-
tions of ca. 800 A.D. and, in the form of a myth, in one of the old
manuscripts from Tun-huang. It has been maintained in the later
(5)



literature independently of the epic (like Ma-ri bka'-'bum and
Gyal-po bka'-thang). This is one of many examples showing
that the author or authors of the epic has or have used various
elements of folk-lore, religious beliefs, ancestral legends and other
components which they could find in the literature and in the oral
tradition.
The decision to send a god as king for men is made in heaven
or in a buddhist paradise by an assembly holding a council. Most
of the written versions maintain side by side two kinds of super-
natural beings responsible for the decision. One is properly lamais.
tic (especially rnying-ma-pa and bka'-brgyud-pa), Padmasambhava
playing and eminent part as incarnated in Ge-sar and great gods
of the tantric pantheon acting as parents. The other, more directly
active, belongs to the more general buddhist pantheon. The god
whg sends down one of his three sons as king for men is either
Tsbangs-pa dkar-po (White Brahmā) or brGya-byin (Indra). On a
third level, more "popular" or indegenous, the hero has so to say a
second father, the holy mountain of gLing, ealled Ge-'dzo, Ger-dzo
(and other related forms), who is defined as the "God of the (Divine)
Person" (sku-lha). : This conception too is already known in the
ancient documents of the royal period where this god appears as
the protector of the kings (sku-bla), also related simultaneously to
the ancestor and the holy mountaio. In the epic, the hero is more-
over protected and guided by his heavenly aunt (Ma ne-ne) Gung-
sman rgyal-mo "Queen Fairy of Heaven" who advises bim what to
do id critical situations. He is also helped by his (4 or 5) brothers
and sister, born together with him under the form of gods with
animal features or as animals made of different metals. Finally he
has as faithful companion his supernatural horse, a kyang (wild ass)
(6)



as well as a boy or girl-friend in each of the countries he is destined
to subdue with their help.
The earthly father is Seng-blon, the old and feebie chief of the
country, the mother 'Gog-tsha Tha-mo having been found during a
hunt. The father's brother, the uncle of the hero, (A-khu) Khro-
thung ("Easily Angry" or "Short-tempered") is a Bon-po disposing
of magical powers but a coward perpetually ridiculised. He is the
main opponent of the hero, wants to be king and to marry the
beautiful girl destined to be the hero's wife, 'Brug-ino.
A long chapter ('Khrungs-gling) tells the miraculous birth of the
hero in the ugly and ridiculous form of Jo-ru, bis bannishment,
together with his mother, to a wild country (in the bend of the
Ma-chu (Yellow River), near the sacred mountain [Ma-chen spom-
ra) where he finds his horse, a shabby and feeble foal. The next
chapter (rta-rgyug) deals with a horse-race decided by the assembly
of gLing : the winner is to be elected king and shall receive 'Brug-
mo as his wife,
All members of the tribe, from the powerful and
rich down to the meanest begger have the right to participate in
the race. Contrary to all expectations, Jo-ru wins and is pro-
claimed king. He is then 13 or 15 years old and now assumes all his
glory and the name Ge-sar (other titles being Seng-chen Nor-bu
dGra-'dul "Great Lion, Jewel, Subduer of ennemies').
It is uncertain which chapter should follow. In most versions
it is the story of the victory over the Demon of the North (byang-
bdud) Klu-btsan, also called Khyab-pa lag-ring like in the bon-po
litterature (bDud-gling). In spite of his location in the Byang-thang
by the epic, several independant traditions assure that he lived in



Kong-po under the name A-khyung. His wife is one of Ge-sar's
girl-friends. Me-za 'Bum-skyid (a lady born Me, i.e. from Me-oyag,
located in the Kokonor region) whom he has carried off. After
many adventures during which Ge-sar has first to subdue the
demon's, sister, A-stag Iha-mo (or klu-mo) and the various outer
"Souls" (bla) of the demon (a tree, a lake, etc.), Me-za betrays the
demon, by revealing to Ge-sar the means of killing him. But after
the death of the demon, she has an ambiguous attitude. By means
of a drink of forgetfullness she retains Ge-sar in the demon's
country.
This absence of Ge-sar from gLing introduces one of the best
known chapters to follow, the war against the neighbouring people
of Hor (located more or less in the region of the Shira-Uigur near
Kan-chow in North-west China). This chapter is always divided
into two parts (Hor-stod and smad). In the first part, one of the
three kings of Hor wants to take 'Brug-mo as his wife. He invades
gLing and is able to carry off 'Brug-mo thanks to the treachery of
Uncle Khro-thung. The young warriors of gLing try to oppose
the rape but don't succeed. On the contrary, once more due to
the betrayal of Khro-thung, Ge-sar's half-brother Gya-tsha-who
is unvulnerable except on a little spot on his body-is killed, and
his "soul" is transformed in a bird. In the second part, messager-
birds sent to Ge-sar at last make him remember his country. He
comes back and starts war against Hor. Going first alone, in the
disguise of jugglers, and engaged by a smith whose daughter is one
of his friends, he is able to melt the while stone which is the "soul"
or life (bla-rdo) or the Hor and to subdue the kings.
The atove mentioned chapters seem to be the main part of the
epic or the more or less original kernel. Out of the many other
(8)



chapters of episodes some have certainly been added at a later
stage, imitating the first ones. Some have been written quite
recently by known authors. An example is the 'Jar-gling written
by the 8th Khams-sprul (rnying-ma-pa) Don-brgyud nyi-ma (born
1931) who has also published a book Gesar and his cult (Tashi-
jong, 1970). I have been told that this new country 'Jar stands for
'Jar-man (Germany). A rapid look at it shows that the style is
exactly the same as in the other chapters. Another new work is
the dBus 'chi-bdag cham-la phab-pa'i le'u (“Victory over the Lord
of Death of dBus") by Ven. Khenpo Kalu (Karmapa Rang-byung
Kun-khyab phrin-las; ed. Bir. 1975). This shows how much the
subject of the Ge-sar epic is still a living source of literary crea-
tion. By analogy one can imagine that other chapters of unidenti-
fied authors may also be quite modern (say XIXth century).
Any how, various authors and several manuscripts of the epic
give lists of chapters (some say 18 or 19). Some of these have been
written in order to comply with a traditional list of a cycle of 18
chapters which seem to be independent. This cycle, often quoted
in various manscripts, is called “The Conquest of the 18 great
Castles" (Bod-kyi rdzong-chen bco-brgyad phab-pa). Each of
these chapters deals with the conquest of the castle of a particular
country, conquered by Ge-sar, from which he takes the essence of
cach time a different kind of precious element of material culture, in
order to distribute them among the chiefs of gLing (nor 'gyed): the
castle of horses from Zi-ling (Chinese Hsi-ning), the castle of
turquoises from Kha-che (Kashmir) and so on. Ge-sar bere acts as
a "culture-hero".
The order of the remaining chapters in most lists is the follow-
ing : 'Jang-gling, conquest of 'Jang Sa-tham (region of Li-chiang in
(9)



Yün-nan, China, and the ancient kingdom of Nan-chao); Lho-gling,
or Mon-gling, conquest of the bon-po or mu-stegs-pa (heretical)
king Shing-khri of the Mon; stag-gzing (Iran) which is part of the
18 castles possessing "riches" (nor), but the main theme is the theft
of their seven blue horses. Other chapters concern the “Mongols"
(Sog-gling, the Sog-po A-drag), Zhang-zhung, Sum-pa, Gru-gu
(the "Turks" in the North; a specially interesting chapter because it
combines the old tradition of Ge-sar, king of the Gru-gu, of which
we shall speak later, and Ge-sar of gling), Bera, Mi-nub (seems
to be in Burma). A special and uncertain place is occupied by the
Travel to China (rGya-le'u). Its first part is devoted to adventures
in Me-nyag from where the hero bas to fetch the necessary ngre-
dients to face the dangers in China. It is probably related to the
chapter of Klu-btsan (Dud-gling) since the latter has taken one
of Ge-sar's wife from Me-nyag (Me-bza').
The last or last but one chapter must be the d Myal-gling, where
Ge-sar goes down to hell and argues with Yama in order to rescue
either his mother or his wife 'Brug-mo or else his "girl-friend"
A-stag Iha-mo (according to different versions). In its larger version,
this chapter contains 18 episodes in which Ge.sar takes leave from
the various heroes. It should be followed by an epilogue on Ge-
sar's departure for a paradise. Two manuscripts of the dMyal-gling
have been published. They are nothing else than copies made of a
printed text.
This fact must be underlined. The printed copies are rare. Most
of the available manuscripts are not original drafts but only hand-
written copies of xylographs or other older manuscripts. In no case
do we deal with a kind of first, original manuscript.
( 10 )



We now have to deal with the possible author or authors (or
may be rewriters) and the date of the epic. Many manuscripts and
xylographs are provided with colophons which give useful indi.
cations, not of one author, but of various writers. So also for the
date, we have to distinguish between the oldest date we can reach
for the existence of the epic as a whole and the various dates of
different chapters which have certainly been written during several
centuries. These dates must in turn be distinguished from the possi-
ble dates of isolated elements used in the various chapters.
The best attested mile-stones for the date of the epic as a
complete literary creation are the Mongolian versions of the Geser
Ķban. A printed edition, partly traostated, but already greatly adap-
ted to Mongolian background, has been published in Peking in 1716.
According to Mongolian traditions this version is based on the acti-
vity of story-tellers from the Kokonor who lived in 1630, and the
edition is attributed to one of the Cang-skya khutukhru, probably
the first one (1642-1714). As is well known these lamas played an
important role at the Manchu Court in Peking. At that time Ge-sar
(Geser) had already been conceived as a dgra-lha and protector of
the Manchu dynasty. A Mongolian text of a Geser ritual is dated
1614, other rituals of the bsang-type being already dated from
ca. 1600.
It is thus sure that the epic and the cult of Ge-sar exis-
ted at the latest in the later part of the XVIth century.
Tibetan authors of the XVIIIth centur (Sum-pa mkhan-po,
1707-1788; the 3rd Panchenlama, writing in 1779-1780; the 2nd
Cang-skya, writing in 1740, and Dar-rgyas No-men-han writting
in 1757) witness that at their time several versions of the epic and
traditions about its heroes were already spread over Khams and
Amdo (for instance the 18 different chapters). A little earlier one
( 11 )



finds in the biography of Mi-'gyur rdo-rje (gNam-chos, vol. MA,
173-174) a story about false prophets and grer-ston in the region of
1Cags-mo Hor and Ma-chen sPom-ra (Khams and Amdo), places
which play a significant role in the epic. Some pretended to be
Padmasambhava or Ge-sar. At that occasion Mi-'gyur rdo-rje
(1645-1667 or 1703 ?), who had many visions and revelations, met
the sPo-bo gter-ston bDud-dul rdo-rje of Kha-thog (1615-1675)
who in his turn met the king of gLing (-tshang) and had “dis-
covered” a gter-ma of rMa-chen sPom-ra. These events may well
be connected with the more recent ?) tradition that the kings of
gling claim to be the descendants of Ge-sar's half-brother, rGya-
tsha, and the location of the Ge-sar story in gLing.tshang. Mi-'gyur
rdo-rje's biography (p. 212a) also states that, in 1663, not only was
he already revered by the bKa’-brgyud-pa, the Sa-skya-pa and the
Bon-po (who reigned in the neighbouring country of Be-ri, just
conquered by the Mongol Gushri Khan in 1640), he also "brought
under his power" the inhabitants of the castle "where Ge-sar, sove-
reign of gLing, had lived”. At that date the country of gLing-tshang
had been since long in narrow relations with the kingdom of sDe-
dge. In the beginning of the XVth century, its ecclesiastical chiefs
had received official diploma and seal from the Chinese emperor
(Ming dynasty) on the same level of dignity as the other great
contemporary hierarchs of Tibet, the Sa-skya-pa, the Phag-mo-gru-
pa, the 'Bri-gung-pa and the stag-tshang-pa. The very reliable
historian dPa'-bo gTsug-lag phreng-ba relates these events in
great detail (1545-1565) in complete accordance with the Chinese
Annals. In spite of this he does not say a word of Ge-sar or of
the epic when speaking of gLing-tshang at ca. 1400 A.D. Maybe
his silence is due to some political reason. It does not necessarily
mean that the epic did not yet exist at his time. As we have seen
from the Mongol data, it was known already around 1600 A.D. The
( 12 )



Tibetan versions must have existed a little earlier. 'Brug-pa Kun-
legs (1455-1529) mentions a statue of a-khu Khro-thung opposing
it as “low" to that of the Buddha (his “autobiography", p. 305).
But this may be due to a later addition of the author. Moreover
as we shall see, Khro-thung was already known in another diffe-
rent cycle of lcgends which is earlier than the epic.
Before turning to this older cycle, we may note som: of the
more significant indications we find in the colophons of various
chapters about their authors or editors. The best known are those
of the printed edition of gLing-tshang which gives the first four
chapters of the story. They have been edited under the supervision
of the great rnying-ma-pa polygraphs and gter-ston of the Ris-med
movement in Khams : Jam-dbyangs mkhyen-brtse'i dbang-po
(1820-1892), 'Ju Mi-pham (1846-1912, who also wrote a volume of
rituals of Ge-sar as dgra-lha), mChog-'gyur gling-pa (grer-ston,
1829-1870), 'Gro-'dul dpa'-bo (A-'dzom 'Brug-pa, 1842-1924) and
Las-rab gling-pa (gler-ston, 1856). The compiler says he has used
"ancient books" kept at gling and that he has taken in account
corrections of a certain Bu-tha nor-bu. He also has used different
versions : one of a medium (dpu'-bo) of Hor-khog (region of Ta'o
in Khams), one of the bard (sgrung-mkhan) Ra-gu a-mye and one
from d Bus.
The chapter on Hell (d Myal-gling), a printed edition, has been
written "in Chinese ink” by the bla-ma of 'Dan ('Dan-khog, near
gLingutshang), Chos-kyi dbang-phyug, who has hidden the text as
a gter-ma in a pond in the mGo-log country. It was “discovered"
there by Drag-rtsal rdo-rje, a member of the lineage of gLing.
Upfortunately the name of this gter-ston is not mentioned in the
( 13 )



biographies of gter-ston included in the Rin-chen gter-mdzod and
thus remains unidentified.
Another detailed colophon is that of several manuscripts of the
Hor-gling. This chapter has been published in Hsi-ning (China)
in 1963 (in Tibetan and in Chinese) by several learned Tibetans
(one of them, Tshe-tan zhabs-drung, being the author of a good
dictionary). In their introduction they indicate that this version
is based on the text (sgrung-dpe) in which the specialist of the
epic (sgrung-bshad mkhas-pa) șDe-dge zhabs-drung (the editors
say he was contemporary of Pho-lha-nas who died in 1747) has
collected the traditons of about twenty bards (sgrung-pa) to whom
he had listened, such as Lha-dbang tshe-ring of Nang-chen and
Tshe-ring don-grub of sDe-dge. The editors add that they have
used five different sgrung-dpe, operating a sort of rewriting in the
generally understood Tibetan language that is not in the local
dialects), but preserving some of the words of "ancient language"
(brda-rnying). This clearly shows how many different oral and
written versions existed already in the middle of the XVIIIth
century, how many different authors are responsable for them and
how many rewrittings, amalgams and mixtures have taken place.
Another kind of indications of author or date is to be taken
with caution. They occur in a verson which seems to have been
written at the end of the XIXth century in the region of Hsi-ning
(near sku-'bum) and Kuei-tö (Bya-khyung) situated on the Yellow
River. The Tibetan text is only known through a badly written
manuscript copy containing only the first five chapters (from Birth
to Hor) but lacking the final story. The latter is only available
(from the Tibetan) in the (rather bad) translation of father M.
Hermanns, but has been recently confirmed by a note of Wang Yi-
( 14 )



nuan (1979). Fortunately a Mongolian translation gives the
complete text and has been published in Ulanbator (1959) and
analysed by Damdinsuren (1957).
At the end of the story, in a kind of epilogue (wish follows the
chapter on Hell and also exists as a seperate text), Ge-sar makes
his final arrangements and gives his last will when he is about to
leave the world for a paradise (as a buddha). He defines the role
he has played and recalls his deeds. In order to preserve and pro-
tects his achievements against the possible revenge of the
descendants of his former ennemies, he appoints three men of the
younger generation of gLing for the three main tasks : 1) 'Od-dkar
rgyal-mtshan, son of rGya-tsha Zhal·lu (half-brother of Ge-sar)
receives the throne and the reign:2) Chos-pa (?) Bum-bha (?)s Tong-
Idan, son of Rong-tsha dMar-leb, is enthrusted with the military de-
fense; 3) bla-ma (in Mongolian : bandi-bandhe) Nor-bu chos-'bebs
(or chos-'phel) receives the care of religion and of the transmission
of all that has been said. His father is gLing dPal-ldan Nor-bu sTag-
Tgyal and his grand-father is Khra-rgan (sPyi-dpon), the old man
who knows the origins because he has lived long enough to witness
them. After Ge-sar's departure for a paradise, Nor-bu chos-'bebs
('phel) assembles all the people of gLing, tells them the life story
of Ge-sar, writes down the "examples" (probably dpe) and sings
the final auspicious verses.
The Mongolian scholar Damdiasuren has concluded from this
story that Nor-bu chos-'bebs was a poet (thus translating handi) and
the author of the epic, kind of chronicler contemporary of Ge-sar
whom Damdinsuren belives to have lived in the XIth century. This
conclusion seems to be impossible. The epilogue of this particular
version is not a colophon written by the author of the epic, but
( 15 )



part of the epic itself authentifying the legendary story of Ge-sar
by the fiction that the hero himself has told it as a testament and
has established his succession on the throne, thus enabling the later
kings of gLing-tshang to claim to be the descendants of rGya-tsha.
A similar kind of fiction (may be a model ?) is well known as the
Last Will (bka'-chems) of Srong-btsan sgam-po.
As we have seen, no trace of the epic (as we have it now) can be
found before ca. 1550 (or eventually 1500 A.D.). But precisely at
at that date or a little earlier we find another remarkable cycle of
legends which contains a great amount of elements of our epic and
has been used by the learned editors of the printed version of gLing.
tshang. This is the rLangs Po-ti bse-ru, written to glorify the
rLangs Lha-gzigs family and especially its most glorious member,
the king of Tibet (Ta'i-si) Byang-chub rgyal-mtshan of Phag-mo-
gru (1302-1364, in power since 1354). It is composed of different
parts, some older (ca. 1400 ?). some later, but seems to have reached
final redaction around 1500 A.D. The story is connected with the
final assembly held on the completion of the building of bSam-yas.
According to a prophecy of Padmasambhava, his incarnation is the
saint and siddha rLangs Byang-chub 'dre-bkol, the incarnation of his
lay patron (yon-bdag), the king Khri-srong Ide-btsan, being gLing-
rje Ge-sar, lay patron of the siddha. Another prophecy announces
the Ta'i-si thirteen generations after the Siddha (ca. 300 years). All
Tibetan historians have thus concluded that Byang-chub 'dre-bkol
and gLing-rie Ge-sar have lived about 1050 A.D. The story of Po-ti
bse-ru is presented as a gier-ma, the essence (snying-po) of a certain
Thang-yig sgron-ma (which has some relation with the various thang
-yig of Padmasambhava's story). In this text the main bero is Byang-
chub 'dre-bkol ("Subduer of demons") who like Padmasambhava
subdues various local gods of Tibet and neighbouring countries,
( 16 )



His lay patron Ge-sar of g Ling receives bis instructions and contri-
butes to the submission of demons. He owns several objects, sort of
palladia, and is surrounded by persons who all occur in the Ge-sar
epic (Seng-blon, Khra-rgan, Khro-thung, etc.). But the location of
his kingdom is different from that of the epic. It occupies the
country east of the great bent of the rMa-chu (Yellow River), south
of Hşi-njog (sKu-'bum), from Ma-chen sPom-ra and roughly Re-
rgya dgon-pa in the west up to the Chinese town T'ao.chow (here
spelled Thel-chu), near Co-ne.
It may be stated here that this region was occupied during more
than a century (XIth-XIlth centuries) by a dynasty of Tibetan
(and/or Chiang) chiefs only koown through the Chinese Annals
(of the Sung dynasty). Acording to them the founder was a certain
Chiai-ssu-to (meaning "Son of Buddha", therefore transcribio-
Gyal-sras). This person has been identified with the gLing Ge.
sar of the Po-ti bse-ru by the contemporary Mongolian scholar
Damdinsuren. In the writer's opinion this identification can not
be maintained. But it is well possible that the existence of this
local Tibetan dynasty of Amdo in the XIth century bas contributed
to the formation of a local legendary cycle and/or its connection
with the already known title of Ge-sar of which we shall speak
hearafter. Anyhow, the clan Langs seems to be related to the
tribes (Sum-pa gLang, etc.) which are often mentioned as the old
in habitants of Amdo. As the Po-ti bşe-ru has been written to
glorify this clan, the name of Ge-sar's country, gLing, may well be
due to an etymological interpretation (“the country" of men
'dzam-bn-gling) of the clan or tribe name rLangs/gLang, because
both dames have the same pronounciation in the Amdo dialect.
To come back to the rLangs Po-ti bse-ru, it contains not only
the names of the principal heroes of the Ge-sar epic and the
( 17 )



connection with Padmasambhava, but also other elements of the
epic, the treasures to be distributed, the ancestor legend of the
rLangs (allied to the IDong, the tribe of gLing in the epic) and
various other details. As I said before, some of these elements
have been consciously used in the redaction of the epic by the
learned ecclesiastics of XIXth century who have edited the printed
edition of gLing-tshang (for example, the bla-ma of gling are
Padmasambhava, rLangs-chen dPal-gyi seng-ge, his pupil, and
rLang-ston Byang-chub 'dre-bkol, in chapter 'Khrungs-gling). Other
elements like the names of the heroes and the ancestral legends may
represent a common stock more or less orally and locally trans-
mitted and more or less independently used, first in the Po-ti bse-ru
and later in tbe epic as we have it now. A notable difference is
the figure of Klu-btsan. In the epic, this demon is subdued by Ge-
sar in the North (Byang-thang). In the Po-ti bse-ru, he is subdued,
together with the mountain Kha-ba dkar-po, in the Tsha-ba rong
in south-east Khams (in other independent traditions, as we saw, he
is located in rKong-po).
Tracing back the main sources of the present Ge-sar epic wc
can make a step further and reach the oldest element wbich con-
cerns the very name of Ge-sar, This name occurs precisely in
"hidden texts" (gter-ma) related with Padmasambhava which were
discovered" in the middle of the XIVth century, just a little
earlier than the Po-ti bse-ru. These are the bka'-thang sde-inga,
especially the rGyal-po bka'-thang and the Blon-po bka'-thang "dis-
covered" in 1347 in (and partly some decades latter). Liket he Po-ti
bse-ru they refer to a general assembly held at the time of Padma-
sambhava's presence at the king's court. Their purpose was to
explain the origin of the world, of men (especially Tibet and the
neighbouring courtries), the lineages or clans and the various
( 18 )



traditions. But in those texts Ge-sar is not the king of gLing, but
one of the representatives of the four great neighbour kingdoms
of Tibet, each of them qualified by a particuliar virtue : China in
the East, India in the South, Iran (Ta-zig) in the West and the
Turcs (Gru-gu or Hor) and/or Ge-sar in the North. In this scheme
Ge-sar represents war. He is the “King of armies" (dmag-gi rgyal-
po). Though sometimes connected with the Turcs, his country
normally is called Kbrom or Phrom. In the bka'-thang sde-Inga
this scheme is treated in verses which look like fragments of a lon-
ger (epical ?) poem. The authors of the present epic knew of
course these gter-ma. They have even used it on purpose in one
of the chapters, the war with the Gru-gu, in which Gru-gu Ge-sar
appears as the ennemy og g Ling Ge-sar. The whole scheme of the
Four Great Kings of the World was already known in Tibet long
before. Allusion is made to it (with different narrative elements) in
the Ma-ni bka'-'bum (gter-ma of the XIIth century) in one of Mi.
la ras-pa's songs (XIIth century, if it is accepted as authentic in
its present form) and in a manuscript of Tua-huang (IXth-Xth cen-
tury at the latest). Before these texts in Tibetan, this scheme occurs
(with appropriate names) in Arabic descriptions of the world (IXth-
Xth centuries) and even earlier in buddhist cosmologies preserved
only in Chinese (VIIth century and earlier). But in the latter the
game of Ge-sar does not yet appear.
It would be too long to try to explain how this conception of
the world and the pames of the countries have become known in
Tibet. It is still unknown what really happened.
But it is sure
that Ge-sar is a transcription of the first Greek, and later Turkish
title kaisar (“'king" or "emperor'') and that Khrom, or better
Phrom represents frūm, an Iranian form of the name Rüm that is
Eastern Rome (Byzance) and Turkish Anatolia.
( 19 )



Thus, the Ge-sar epic involves all kinds of problems. Different
elements have been used at different dates and have different origins.
Though all questions are of course interesting, it should not be
forgotten that the epic itself, as a literary master-piece, is worth
while to be studied for its own sake. It is moreover a mine of
information and reflects the Tibetan traditional society as it has lived
for at least the last four centuries. It should be hoped that scholars
would at last deal with it and give it the attention it deserves.
